10.1016/j.jmp.2009.09.003
The analysis of repeated measurements deals with data sets containing experimental units that are measured at multiple time points or under multiple conditions. Throughout this paper, we consider the case where the repeated measurements factor is time, although the statistical analysis of both cases is identical. When analyzing repeated measurements data, researchers often have specific expectations about the changes over time. These expectations can often be translated into inequality and equality constraints between the measurement means at each time point, e.g., the response variable increases on average over time. When the population is divided into different groups, the researcher may also be interested in the difference between these groups over time. For instance, group 1 may respond the same as group 2 during the first measurements but have larger outcomes during the last measurements. Another case of repeated measurements occurs when one is interested in the differences between group means over time adjusted for covariates. A variation is obtained when the covariates vary over time. Finally, combinations of these cases are expected.

As was pointed out by  Davis (2002) , repeated measurements can be modeled in different ways. Here, the multivariate normal linear model will be applied to model the repeated measurements. Let us assume that we observed

These informative hypotheses can be translated into equality and inequality constrained models where the parameter space of the measurement means are constrained according to the constraints in the informative hypotheses. Subsequently, a model selection criterion can be used to select the best of the set of the equality and inequality constrained models.

In this paper, the Bayes factor is used as a selection rule. For a detailed overview of the properties of the Bayes factor, see, for instance,  Kass and Raftery (1995) . In order to use the Bayes factor as selection criterion between a set of equality and inequality constrained models, a prior distribution must be specified for the parameters under each model of interest. When the constrained models under evaluation are nested in a larger encompassing model,  Berger and Mortera (1999)  specified a prior under this encompassing model from which truncated distributions were constructed under the constrained models. Independently, the idea of an encompassing prior was successfully applied by  Klugkist, Laudy, and Hoijtink (2005b)  in the context of analysis of (co)variance. In this paper, we present a generalization of this methodology to address the selection problem in the context of repeated measurements data.

A key difference of the methodology used in this paper in comparison to the encompassing prior approach of Klugkist, Laudy, and Hoijtink is that training data is used to specify the prior distribution. A training sample is a small part of the data which can be used to update a noninformative improper prior into a proper posterior prior. A training sample is called minimal if no subset results in a proper posterior prior. Subsequently, the posterior prior is used as prior distribution for the model parameters. The use of training data is a formal method to obtain objective (or default) priors with minimal information such that subjective prior specification is avoided. For a detailed discussion of the use of training data to obtain so-called default Bayes factors, see, for instance,  Berger and Pericchi (1996, 2004)  and  Pérez and Berger (2002) .

In Section  2 , an example from developmental psychology is used to describe four different situations in the context of repeated measurements where it is the goal to select the best of a set of inequality and equality constrained models which are based on informative hypotheses. Section  3  will elaborate how the Bayes factor can be used to select the best of a set of constrained models. This is followed by a simulation study in Section  4  which will illustrate the performance of the proposed method. In Section  5 , the informative hypotheses discussed in Section  2  are evaluated using the proposed methodology. The paper is concluded with a short discussion and five appendices containing the technical details.

As an illustration for the evaluation of informative hypotheses in the context of repeated measurements, we use part of a larger data set described in  Selfhout, Branje, and Meeus (2009) , that resulted from the CONflict And Management Of RElationships study CONAMORE; ( Meeus et al., 2004 ). CONAMORE is an ongoing longitudinal study in the Netherlands, and the main aim is to asses relationships of adolescents with parents and best friends, as well as the emotional states of the participants. We will use the questionnaire data of five annual waves. Included in our analyses are Dutch participants who were 13 years old at the first wave ( 
                            N 
                            = 
                            340 
                         ). The outcome variable examined in the illustrations is self-reported satisfaction with peer relations. Perceived satisfaction was assessed with the commitment scale of the Investment Model Scale ( Rusbult, Martz, & Agnew, 1998 ). This scale contains 4 items (e.g. “I want the relationship with my best friend to stay good”), measured on a 5-point scale (1 = absolutely disagree, 5 = absolutely agree). This variable was measured at each wave and therefore we are able to examine the development of perceived satisfaction with peer relations from early adolescence (13 years) until late adolescence (17 years).

The first specification of the general repeated measures model in  (1)  is the one within the factor model. In this model the outcome variable is measured on several occasions within one sample. For the illustrative data, the statistical model for respondent

Perceived satisfaction of the relationship with the adolescent’s best friend is believed to be relatively low in early adolescence compared to middle and late adolescence and increases over time. Theories concerning connectedness and individuation also suggest that normative development across adolescence is characterized by increases in bonding between adolescents and their parents and peers (e.g.  Grotevant & Cooper, 1998 ). Therefore, the first informative hypothesis states that the perceived satisfaction increases from early adolescence onwards.

Selman (1990)  suggests that during late childhood and early adolescence, perceived satisfaction is expressed by the sharing of experiences with a regard for the other’s opinion, but still primarily for the sake of each persons own satisfaction. Therefore, perceived closeness is suggested to remain relatively low in early adolescence compared to middle and late adolescence. From middle adolescence onwards, closeness increasingly involves sharing each others feelings and thoughts. Therefore, the second hypothesis states that the increase over time starts slowly and becomes more pronounced when the adolescent becomes older. An extension of the previous design is the within–between model. In these designs the main interest is the investigation of possible differences in development over time between two or more subgroups, for instance, comparing the short and long-term effects of several treatments. Previous research indicates that girls have been shown to perceive higher closeness (e.g.,  Furman and Buhrmester (1992)  and  Tse and Cheng (2006) ) and higher balanced relatedness ( Shulman & Knafo, 1997 ) with their peers than boys, suggesting that girls perceive higher friendship quality. This could suggest that girls also perceive more satisfaction from peer relations than boys do. Even though the literature clearly suggests that girls experience more closeness in adolescence than boys do, the timing of when these differences arise in adolescence has not been examined yet. We therefore examine the hypothesis that at age 14 there are not yet any differences between boys and girls, after age 14 girls perceive more satisfaction than boys, and the difference between boys and girls gets more pronounced when the adolescents become older. Following up on the previous examples, it is believed that the adolescent’s appraisal of marital conflict has an influence on the perceived satisfaction of the relation with the adolescent’s best friend ( Fauber, Forehand, Thomas, & Wierson, 1990 ). Comparing the satisfaction scores of boys and girls controlling for the possible effect of appraisal of marital conflict is done by including the latter as a time invariant covariate. The appraisal of marital conflict was assessed with the questionnaire of  Grych and Fincham (1993) . This scale contains 5 items (e.g., “How often do your parents quarrel about money as far as you know?”), measured on a 5-point scale (1 = never, 5 = very often) so that the resulting scores are on a scale from 5 to 25. This covariate is standardized so that the intercepts in the model  (1)  become the adjusted means of perceived satisfaction corrected for the effect of appraisal of marital conflict. The correction for a covariate is illustrated in  Fig. 3 
                          using hypothetical data. The  
                            y 
                         -axis represents satisfaction at one measurement occasion (e.g. age 13) and the  
                            x 
                         -axis appraisal of marital conflict. Scores are represented by open (boys) and solid (girls) circles and the relation between appraisal and satisfaction by linear regression lines.

If the covariate is not taken into account, the mean satisfaction scores for boys and girls are compared. In the figure, these means are denoted by

                         , respectively. Including a correction for conflict awareness, i.e., including appraisal of marital conflict as a covariate, leads to the comparison of so-called adjusted means. These are the mean satisfaction scores per subgroup evaluated at the overall mean score on the appraisal variable, which equals zero after standardization. The adjusted means are denoted

Note again that  Fig. 3  is based on hypothetical data and not the real CONAMORE data to better illustrate the possible effect of inclusion of a covariate.

Although  Fig. 3  presents the satisfaction scores and the relation between appraisal and satisfaction at one measurement occasion, the inclusion of a covariate in a repeated measures analysis compares covariate adjusted means at all measurements. In the final example, mean satisfaction scores for boys and girls are compared after correction for generalized anxiety disorder. This variable was assessed with one of the sub scales of the revised version of the Screen for Child Anxiety Related Emotional Disorders (SCARED-R;  Muris & Steerneman, 2001 ). The sub scale contains 7 items (e.g. “I worry a lot whether people like me”), measured on a 3-point scale (1 = almost never, 3 = often), i.e., the scores on this scale range from 7 to 21. Previous research suggests that experienced emotional problems, such as generalized anxiety disorder, are related to perceived satisfaction ( Burwell & Shirk, 2007; Rose, Carlson, & Waller, 2007 ). Moreover, gender differences on anxiety are expected. First of all, girls are known to experience more anxiety than boys ( Bernstein, Borchardt, & Perwien, 1996 ). Besides this, girls who already experience emotional problems tend to encourage each other to focus more on their emotional problems and experience a negative affect within their friendships. This, so called, co-rumination, in turn, has been shown to be related with increases in anxiety over time ( Rose et al., 2007 ).

In the sequel, we will evaluate the hypotheses formulated in Section  2.3 , now controlling for generalized anxiety disorder measured at each wave  
                            t 
                            = 
                            13 
                            , 
                            … 
                            , 
                            17 
                         .

Different model selection criteria have been proposed in the literature, such as the AIC ( Akaike, 1973 ), the BIC or Schwarz criterion ( Schwarz, 1978 ), and the DIC ( Spiegelhalter, Best, Carlin, & van der Linde, 2002 ). Another useful reference for model selection criteria for informative hypotheses is the edited volume by  Hoijtink, Klugkist, and Boelen (2008) .

Generally, model selection criteria behave like an ‘Occam’s razor’, which means that they consist of a ‘fit’ part and a ‘complexity’ part. As a consequence, a criterion considers the simplest model to be the best when two models fit the data equally well. However, the notion of model complexity is approached from different perspectives for different selection criteria (e.g.  Myung, 2000 ).

                      , the number of free parameters is unclear, and therefore, the application of the AIC and BIC cannot be recommended.

A modification of the AIC has been proposed by  Anraku (1999)  with an adjusted penalty for model complexity for models containing simple order restrictions in the case of the analysis of variance. Anraku referred to this information criterion as the order restricted information criterion (ORIC). For data where the likelihood is completely located in the inequality constrained space of model  
                         
                            
                               M 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                      , the measure of fit of both models is equal. However, the ORIC prefers the inequality constrained model  
                         
                            
                               M 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                       over the unconstrained model  
                         
                            
                               M 
                            
                            
                               0 
                            
                         
                       as a result of a smaller penalty for model complexity for model  
                         
                            
                               M 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                      . Hence, when the inequality constraint of model  
                         
                            
                               M 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                       is supported by the data, model  
                         
                            
                               M 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                       is preferred over model  
                         
                            
                               M 
                            
                            
                               0 
                            
                         
                       because model  
                         
                            
                               M 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                       contains more information about the underlying relationship of the model parameters than model  
                         
                            
                               M 
                            
                            
                               0 
                            
                         
                      . This selection behavior corresponds with our intuition.

For the DIC, the effective number of parameters  
                         
                            
                               p 
                            
                            
                               D 
                            
                         
                       is calculated using the posterior distribution as can be seen in  (2) . For data where the likelihood, and therefore the posterior, is completely located in the inequality constrained space of model  
                         
                            
                               M 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                      , the posterior estimates are identical under both models  
                         
                            
                               M 
                            
                            
                               0 
                            
                         
                       and  
                         
                            
                               M 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                      . Consequently, the DIC is equal for both models, although the data supports the inequality constraint of model  
                         
                            
                               M 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                      . This conflicts with the Occam’s razor of preferring model  
                         
                            
                               M 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                       instead of model  
                         
                            
                               M 
                            
                            
                               0 
                            
                         
                       when both models fit the data equally well. For this reason, we do not recommend the use of the DIC for selecting between inequality constrained models.

A more exploratory approach was proposed by  Stern (2005)  when the interest is in simple ordering of the group means. In the case of four groups, Stern suggested to calculate the posterior probabilities of all 24 possible orderings (using a noninformative improper prior) instead of only considering the inequality constrained models that make sense to the applied researcher. Note that this criterion is only a measure of goodness-of-fit and does not incorporate model complexity. As a consequence, it only makes sense to use such a criterion when the models are equally complex, and therefore automatically, non-nested. For example, the posterior probability that the inequality constraints of model  
                         
                            
                               M 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                         : 
                         
                            
                               μ 
                            
                            
                               13 
                            
                         
                         < 
                         ⋯ 
                         < 
                         
                            
                               μ 
                            
                            
                               17 
                            
                         
                       (Section  2.2 ) hold is always larger than the posterior probability that the inequality constraints of model  
                         
                            
                               M 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                         : 
                         0 
                         < 
                         
                            
                               μ 
                            
                            
                               14 
                            
                         
                         − 
                         
                            
                               μ 
                            
                            
                               13 
                            
                         
                         < 
                         ⋯ 
                         < 
                         
                            
                               μ 
                            
                            
                               17 
                            
                         
                         − 
                         
                            
                               μ 
                            
                            
                               16 
                            
                         
                       hold because model  
                         
                            
                               M 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                       is nested in model  
                         
                            
                               M 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                      . For this reason, these probabilities do not provide us an answer to whether model  
                         
                            
                               M 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                       should be preferred over model  
                         
                            
                               M 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                      .

Furthermore, the informative hypotheses from Section  2  contain equalities, and therefore, the posterior probability that these constraints hold are zero by definition. Also, when the differences between parameters are also of interest, such as model  
                         
                            
                               M 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                         : 
                         0 
                         < 
                         
                            
                               μ 
                            
                            
                               14 
                            
                         
                         − 
                         
                            
                               μ 
                            
                            
                               13 
                            
                         
                         < 
                         ⋯ 
                         < 
                         
                            
                               μ 
                            
                            
                               17 
                            
                         
                         − 
                         
                            
                               μ 
                            
                            
                               16 
                            
                         
                       (Section  2.2 ), the total number of constrained models to analyze may become too large for practical assessment. See also  Klugkist, Laudy, and Hoijtink (2005a)  for a detailed response to  Stern (2005) .

For this reason, we choose a confirmatory approach using the Bayes factor as a selection criterion for informative hypotheses which is discussed in the following section.

                          This expression shows that the posterior is a weighted average between the prior and the likelihood. The conditional posterior of the covariance matrix  
                            
                               Σ 
                            
                          given  
                            
                               μ 
                            
                          has an inverse-Wishart distribution which is expressed in  (37)  in  Appendix D  for the general normal linear model (as well as for  
                            
                               μ 
                            
                            | 
                            
                               Σ 
                            
                            , 
                            
                               Y 
                            
                         ). The conditional distributions are used in the Gibbs sampler to obtain posterior samples which are used to calculate the Bayes factor. This will be elaborated later in this section.

In the sequel it will be shown that the Bayes factor has an incorporated Occam’s razor, which means that it combines model complexity and model fit to select the best model. Furthermore, we will illustrate how the model complexity of inequality constrained models is taken into account. Subsequently, we illustrate how a default proper prior can be obtained resulting in Bayes factors that appropriately balance the fit and complexity of informative hypotheses. At the end of this section, we briefly discuss how to obtain the Bayes factor using a Gibbs sampler.

Expression  (7)  shows that noninformative improper priors cannot be used for calculating Bayes factors because the outcome will depend on the undefined constants in these priors. This result dates back to  Jeffreys (1961) . In the following section we discuss how a proper prior can be obtained resulting in Bayes factors with desirable properties.

The Bayes factor in  (7)  can be interpreted as the amount of evidence in favor of model

Expression  (9)  showed that the Bayes factor of an inequality constrained model

As was discussed at the beginning of the previous section, a proper prior must be used to calculate the Bayes factor. For this reason, noninformative improper priors, e.g., reference priors ( Berger, Bernardo, & Sun, 2009 ), cannot be used. To avoid subjective prior specification, different methods have been proposed to construct a default or automatic Bayes factor, such as the intrinsic Bayes factor ( Berger & Pericchi, 1996, 2004 ) and the Bayes factor based on the expected-posterior prior ( Pérez & Berger, 2002 ). These methods share the idea that a subset of the data is used for the construction of the prior distribution. Such a data set is also referred to as a training sample and the obtained prior distribution is called a posterior prior. When using the smallest possible subset of the data that results in a posterior prior that is proper, the training sample is called minimal. Generally, the use of minimal training samples is recommended so that the data maximally dominate the posterior prior. Although the idea of a prior based on training data may seem counter intuitive, it can be seen as a formal way of constructing a relatively noninformative prior that is located around the likelihood of the data. Furthermore, Lindley’s paradox ( Bartlett, 1957; Lindley, 1957 ) is avoided because such a prior is not arbitrarily specified. In the remaining part of this section, we discuss how a default prior can be obtained based on minimal training samples that results in Bayes factors that can effectively be used for testing informative hypotheses.

Restricted regression combines data with linear constraints on the parameters such that the resulting parameter estimates satisfy the linear constraints. In restricted regression, the linear restrictions are interpreted as prior information. In our approach, the linear restrictions are simply incorporated such that a posterior prior can be constructed with desirable properties.

Another useful property of constrained posterior priors is that they are more robust to outliers than standard posterior priors as a result of the additional linear restrictions. As was displayed in  Fig. 6 , the restrictions  
                            
                               
                                  μ 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                            = 
                            
                               
                                  μ 
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                            
                            = 
                            
                               
                                  μ 
                               
                               
                                  3 
                               
                            
                          shift the constrained posterior prior means towards the diagonal and result in larger prior variances of the  
                            μ 
                         ’s. For this reason, the influence of outliers in a randomly selected training sample becomes less in comparison to standard posterior priors.

The only issue that remains is the dependence of the constrained posterior prior on the randomly chosen training sample, which influences the corresponding posterior, and therefore, the resulting Bayes factor. To resolve this issue we propose a similar method as the empirical expected-posterior prior (EEPP) of  Pérez and Berger (2002) . The idea is that a single prior is constructed by taking the arithmetic average of all possible constrained posterior priors. This results in a prior that (i) contains minimal information, (ii) is more robust to outliers than standard posterior priors due to the additional linear restrictions, (iii) produces a Bayes factor that appropriately balances model fit and model complexity for a large class of informative hypotheses (as will be seen in the simulations in Section  5 ), (iv) can be constructed without additional (subjective) information, and (v) provides a Bayes factor that is relatively inexpensive in terms of computation time, which is a big problem for large models. Some might object to the idea of using the data twice: once for prior specification and once for model comparison. We believe that this problem is marginal because this prior contains minimal information. Furthermore, we note that the Bayes factor is related to the update of the prior into the posterior. This updating results in evidence in favor of or against the hypothesis under investigation. From our point of view, it makes sense that the starting point of this updating, i.e., the prior, is based on a training sample that contains minimal information because this results in a relative measure of how much evidence there is in favor or against a hypothesis relative to a sample from the same population containing minimal information.

Hence, we can construct the empirical expected-constrained posterior prior (EECPP) under the unconstrained model  
                            
                               
                                  M 
                               
                               
                                  0

                            L 
                          minimal training samples, i.e.,

When using the CECPP, the Bayes factor of an inequality constrained model

                                  q 
                               
                            
                         . In  Appendix D , it is also described how the multivariate normal linear model in  (1)  can be used to model time-varying covariates using restricted regression.

For equality constrained models, this technique cannot be directly utilized because sampled values are never exactly equal. In  Appendix E , it is described how to determine the Bayes factor of a constrained model  
                            
                               
                                  M 
                               
                               
                                  q 
                               
                            
                          containing equality (and possibly inequality) constraints versus the unconstrained model  
                            
                               
                                  M 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                         .

The first set of hypotheses with respect to the CONAMORE data concerns the development of perceived satisfaction of peer relations in adolescents from 13 to 17 years. In the last column of  Table 3 
                         , the sample means and standard deviations are provided for the perceived satisfaction scores in each of the five waves.

In  Table 4 
                         , the three models that were formulated earlier are presented. The Bayes factor for each of these models versus the unconstrained model  
                            
                               
                                  M 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                          is computed and provided in the second column of  Table 4 . As can be seen, all three Bayes factor values are larger than one, implying support for each constrained model. The interpretation of these results is, for instance, that the constrained model  
                            
                               
                                  M 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                          is approximately 42 times better than the unconstrained model  
                            
                               
                                  M 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                         .

From these Bayes factors, Bayes factors between constrained models can be computed. For example, the Bayes factor of  
                            
                               
                                  M 
                               
                               
                                  3 
                               
                            
                          versus  
                            
                               
                                  M 
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                            
                          is obtained by:  
                            
                               
                                  B 
                               
                               
                                  32 
                               
                            
                            = 
                            
                               
                                  B 
                               
                               
                                  30 
                               
                            
                            / 
                            
                               
                                  B 
                               
                               
                                  20 
                               
                            
                            = 
                            60.7 
                            / 
                            14.0 
                            = 
                            4.3 
                          suggesting that model  
                            
                               
                                  M 
                               
                               
                                  3 
                               
                            
                          is approximately four times better than model  
                            
                               
                                  M 
                               
                               
                                  2 
                               
                            
                         . From such calculations, it can be concluded that model  
                            
                               
                                  M 
                               
                               
                                  3 
                               
                            
                          receives most evidence from the data. However, model  
                            
                               
                                  M 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                          receives approximately an equal amount of evidence from the data because  
                            
                               
                                  B 
                               
                               
                                  31 
                               
                            
                            = 
                            1.4 
                         .

In sum, the hypothesis that assumes no change of perceived satisfaction of peer relations in adolescents from 13 to 17 years ( 
                            
                               
                                  M 
                               
                               
                                  3 
                               
                            
                         ) and the hypothesis that assumes an increase of perceived satisfaction ( 
                            
                               
                                  M 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                         ) receive strong support from the data where the first slightly outperforms the latter. For this reason, both hypotheses are used in the sequel when also analyzing the effect of gender on the perceived satisfaction over time.

This illustration investigates the difference between boys and girls on the development of perceived satisfaction. In  Table 3 , sample means and standard deviations are provided for the perceived satisfaction scores at five waves for boys and girls separately. As was stated earlier, we planned to add four different gender hypotheses to the ‘development-over-time’ hypothesis that received the strongest support in the previous analysis. In this illustration, the same models are evaluated as in the previous analysis, but now we control for the time-invariant covariate ‘awareness of marital conflict’, which is measured at the first wave. Hence, a different research question is addressed because now perceived satisfaction is compared between adolescents with an equal score for their awareness of marital conflict. In  Table 3 , the sample means and standard deviations of this variable are provided. This covariate is standardized prior to the calculation of the Bayes factors so that the model constraints are imposed on the adjusted means. The results are presented in  Table 5  in the column under ‘Analysis 3’.

Hence, it can be concluded that two hypotheses receive strong evidence based on the peer satisfaction adjusted for awareness of marital conflict. The hypothesis that assumes that peer satisfaction increases over time and that girls receive more peer satisfaction than boys receives most support. The support for the hypothesis that assumes that this gender difference becomes more pronounced as adolescents become older is slightly less. Hence, the hypothesis that receives most support in this illustration is different from the previous illustration where awareness of marital conflict was not taken into account.

In the last analysis, the same six models are evaluated, now controlling for the time-varying covariate ‘generalized anxiety disorder’ which is measured at each wave. In  Table 3 , the sample means and standard deviations of this variable are provided. Again, this covariate is standardized prior to the calculation of the Bayes factors. In  Table 5  the results are given in the column labeled ‘Analysis 4’.

This table shows that model  
                            
                               
                                  M 
                               
                               
                                  32 
                               
                            
                          receives the strongest support from the data with  
                            
                               
                                  B 
                               
                               
                                  32 
                                  , 
                                  0 
                               
                            
                            = 
                            111052.6 
                         . This model assumes that, after controlling for generalized anxiety disorder, the average level of satisfaction is stable over time and that the profile of girls is higher than boys.

Hence, when controlling for generalized anxiety disorder at each wave, there is strong evidence that peer satisfaction is stable over time and that girls tend to qualify their relation with peers higher than boys.

The four different analyses that were performed in this section showed that it is important for an applied researcher to think about which covariates to include in the analysis. As was observed, the inclusion of different covariates may result in different results concerning the hypothesis that is most supported from the data.

Furthermore, it is important whether or not to combine hypotheses that focus on different facets of the repeated measurements. For the data that was analyzed in this section, we combined hypotheses that were concerned with the development of satisfaction with peers over time with hypotheses that were concerned with gender differences at each wave. Such choices result in different hypotheses, and therefore, result in answers to different research questions.

In practice, experts often have different expectations about the relationship between measurement means over time in a repeated measurement experiment. These expectations can be formulated using equality and inequality constraints among the measurement means. In this paper, we showed how the Bayes factor can be used to determine the support in the data for each expectation.

The use of the conjugate expected-constrained posterior prior (CECPP), which is based on minimal training samples and specific restrictions on the measurement means, results in ‘balanced’ Bayes factors concerning model fit and model complexity. This is an attractive property because it rewards precisely specified models in comparison to vaguely specified models when the data fits both models equally well. Three simulation studies showed that the approach performs adequately when the goal is to determine the support for each model in the set of constrained models. The CONAMORE data were used to illustrate the applicability of the methodology in empirical research.

The authors wish to thank Jay Myung, Charles Liu, and an unknown reviewer for their helpful comments which improved the manuscript. This research was supported by grant NWO-VICI-453-05-002 of the Netherlands Organization for Scientific Research.

Based on these model constraints, linear restrictions are constructed for the model parameters which are necessary to obtain constrained posterior priors (CPPs) that result in Bayes factors that appropriately incorporate the complexity of inequality constrained models. The restrictions ensure that specific  
                         μ 
                      ’s have equal prior distributions in the CPP. The parameters that should have equal priors are the  
                         μ 
                      ’s that are located in the same (in)equality constraint in  (3) . Hence, a constraint of the form  
                         
                            ( 
                            1 
                            , 
                            − 
                            1 
                            , 
                            0 
                            , 
                            … 
                            , 
                            0

                            1 
                            , 
                            − 
                            1

                            0

                            0 
                            ) 
                         
                      , a constraint of the form  
                         
                            ( 
                            − 
                            1

                            2

                            − 
                            1

                            0 
                            , 
                            … 
                            , 
                            0 
                            )

Similar to the empirical expected-posterior prior (EEPP) of Berger and Pérez (2002), the empirical expected-constrained posterior prior (EECPP) can be obtained by averaging over all possible CPP densities:  
                         
                            (32)

                       Although the CPPs each have a normal-inverse-Wishart distribution (see  Appendix B ), which is the conjugate prior for the multivariate normal linear model (e.g.  Press, 2005 , p. 252–257), this is not the case for the EECPP. For this reason, the EECPP is approximated by a normal-inverse-Wishart distribution, which is referred to as the conjugate expected-constrained posterior prior (CECPP). This can be done by sampling from the EECPP and estimating the hyperparameters of the normal-inverse-Wishart CECPP from the obtained sample. A sample of size  
                         M 
                       from the EECPP can be obtained as follows:
